srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Empty_Star.svg/23px-Empty_Star.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Empty_Star.svg/30px-Empty_Star.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="text-align:right; padding-right:0.5em;">Induction</th><td style="padding-left:0.5em;">\n<a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_1944" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 1944">1944</a></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><th colspan="2">\n</th></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Kenesaw Mountain Landis</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xc9\x94\xcb\x90/: &#39;au&#39; in &#39;fraud&#39;">\xc9\x94\xcb\x90</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/a\xca\x8a/: &#39;ou&#39; in &#39;mouth&#39;">a\xca\x8a</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span></span> <wbr /><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/\xc3\xa6/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;bad&#39;">\xc3\xa6</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>; November 20, 1866&#160;\xe2\x80\x93 November 25, 1944) was an American <a href="/wiki/Jurist" title="Jurist">jurist</a> who served as a <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_judge" title="United States federal judge">federal judge</a> from 1905 to 1922 and as the first <a href="/wiki/Commissioner_of_Baseball" title="Commissioner of Baseball">Commissioner of Baseball</a> from 1920 until his death. He is remembered for his handling of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sox scandal">Black Sox scandal</a>, in which he expelled eight members of the <a href="/wiki/1919_Chicago_White_Sox_season" title="1919 Chicago White Sox season">Chicago White Sox</a> from <a href="/wiki/Organized_baseball" class="mw-redirect" title="Organized baseball">organized baseball</a> for conspiring to lose the <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a> and repeatedly refused their reinstatement requests.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> His firm actions and iron rule over baseball in the near quarter-century of his commissionership are generally credited with restoring public confidence in the game.\n</p><p>Landis was born in <a href="/wiki/Millville,_Ohio" title="Millville, Ohio">Millville, Ohio</a>, in 1866. His name was a spelling variation on the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kennesaw_Mountain" title="Battle of Kennesaw Mountain">Battle of Kennesaw Mountain</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, where his father was wounded in 1864. Landis spent much of his youth in Indiana; he left school at fifteen and worked in a series of positions in that state. His involvement in politics led to a civil service job. At age 21, Landis applied to become a lawyer\xe2\x80\x94there were then no educational or examination requirements for the Indiana bar. Following a year of unprofitable practice, he went to <a href="/wiki/Law_school" title="Law school">law school</a>. After his graduation, he opened an office in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, but left it when <a href="/wiki/Walter_Q._Gresham" title="Walter Q. Gresham">Walter Q. Gresham</a>, the new <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">United States Secretary of State</a>, named him his personal secretary in 1893. After Gresham\'s death in 1895, Landis refused an offer of an <a href="/wiki/Ambassador" title="Ambassador">ambassadorship</a>, and returned to Chicago to practice law and marry.\n</p><p>President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> appointed Landis a federal judge in 1905. Landis received national attention in 1907 when he fined <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_of_Indiana" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Oil of Indiana">Standard Oil of Indiana</a> more than $29&#160;million for violating federal laws forbidding rebates on railroad freight tariffs. Though Landis was reversed on appeal, he was seen as a judge determined to rein in <a href="/wiki/Big_business" title="Big business">big business</a>. During and after World War I, Landis presided over several high-profile trials of draft resisters and others whom he saw as opposing the war effort. He imposed heavy sentences on those who were convicted; some of the convictions were reversed on appeal, and other sentences were commuted.\n</p><p>In 1920, Judge Landis was a leading candidate when <a href="/wiki/American_League" title="American League">American League</a> and <a href="/wiki/National_League" title="National League">National League</a> team owners, embarrassed by the Black Sox scandal and other instances of players throwing games, sought someone to rule over baseball. Landis was given full power to act in the sport\'s best interest, and used that power extensively over the next quarter-century. Landis was widely praised for cleaning up the game, although some of his decisions in the Black Sox matter remain controversial: supporters of <a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe_Jackson" title="Shoeless Joe Jackson">"Shoeless Joe" Jackson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buck_Weaver" title="Buck Weaver">Buck Weaver</a> contend that he was overly harsh with those players. Others blame Landis for, in their view, delaying the <a href="/wiki/Baseball_color_line" title="Baseball color line">racial integration of baseball</a>. Landis was elected to the <a href="/wiki/National_Baseball_Hall_of_Fame" class="mw-redirect" title="National Baseball Hall of Fame">National Baseball Hall of Fame</a> by <a href="/wiki/Baseball_Hall_of_Fame_balloting,_1944" title="Baseball Hall of Fame balloting, 1944">a special vote</a> shortly after he died in 1944.\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Early_life_and_pre-judicial_career_(1866\xe2\x80\x931905)"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Early life and pre-judicial career (1866\xe2\x80\x931905)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Boyhood_and_early_career_(1866\xe2\x80\x9393)"><span class="tocnumber">1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Boyhood and early career (1866\xe2\x80\x9393)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Washington_years_and_aftermath_(1893\xe2\x80\x931905)"><span class="tocnumber">1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Washington years and aftermath (1893\xe2\x80\x931905)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-4"><a href="#Judge_(1905\xe2\x80\x9322)"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Judge (1905\xe2\x80\x9322)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#Standard_Oil_(1905\xe2\x80\x9309)"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Standard Oil (1905\xe2\x80\x9309)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Federal_League_and_Baby_Iraene_cases_(1909\xe2\x80\x9317)"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Federal League and Baby Iraene cases (1909\xe2\x80\x9317)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Wartime_cases_(1917\xe2\x80\x9319)"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Wartime cases (1917\xe2\x80\x9319)</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Building_trades_award,_controversy,_and_resignation_(1920\xe2\x80\x9322)"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Building trades award, controversy, and resignation (1920\xe2\x80\x9322)</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Commissioner_(1920\xe2\x80\x9344)"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Commissioner (1920\xe2\x80\x9344)</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Appointment"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Appointment</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-11"><a href="#Black_Sox_scandal"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Black Sox scandal</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-12"><a href="#Search_for_a_commissioner"><span class="tocnumber">3.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Search for a commissioner</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Establishing_control"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Establishing control</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-14"><a href="#Banning_the_Black_Sox"><span class="tocnumber">3.2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Banning the Black Sox</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-15"><a href="#Cracking_down_on_gambling"><span class="tocnumber">3.2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Cracking down on gambling</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#Ruth-Meusel_barnstorming_incident"><span class="tocnumber">3.2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Ruth-Meusel barnstorming incident</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Policies_as_commissioner"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Policies as commissioner</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Major-minor_league_relations;_development_of_the_farm_system"><span class="tocnumber">3.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Major-minor league relations; development of the farm system</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-19"><a href="#Baseball_color_line"><span class="tocnumber">3.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Baseball color line</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-20"><a href="#World_Series_and_All-Star_Game;_other_innovations"><span class="tocnumber">3.3.3</span> <span class="toctext">World Series and All-Star Game; other innovations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#World_War_II,_death,_and_legacy"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">World War II, death, and legacy</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-23"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span id="Early_life_and_pre-judicial_career_.281866.E2.80.931905.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_pre-judicial_career_(1866\xe2\x80\x931905)">Early life and pre-judicial career (1866\xe2\x80\x931905)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and pre-judicial career (1866\xe2\x80\x931905)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Boyhood_and_early_career_.281866.E2.80.9393.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Boyhood_and_early_career_(1866\xe2\x80\x9393)">Boyhood and early career (1866\xe2\x80\x9393)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Boyhood and early career (1866\xe2\x80\x9393)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_boys.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Landis_boys.png/220px-Landis_boys.png" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Landis_boys.png/330px-Landis_boys.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Landis_boys.png/440px-Landis_boys.png 2x" data-file-width="2208" data-file-height="1656" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_boys.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The five Landis boys in November 1882; Kenesaw (second from left) was almost sixteen years old.</div></div></div>\n<p>Kenesaw Mountain Landis was born in <a href="/wiki/Millville,_Ohio" title="Millville, Ohio">Millville, Ohio</a>, the sixth child and fourth son of Abraham Hoch Landis, a physician, and Mary Kumler Landis, on November 20, 1866. The Landises descended from Swiss <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" class="mw-redirect" title="Mennonite">Mennonites</a> who had emigrated to <a href="/wiki/Alsace" title="Alsace">Alsace</a> before coming to the United States. Abraham Landis had been wounded fighting on the Union side at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kennesaw_Mountain" title="Battle of Kennesaw Mountain">Battle of Kennesaw Mountain</a> in Georgia, and when his parents proved unable to agree on a name for the new baby, Mary Landis proposed that they call him Kenesaw Mountain. At the time, both spellings of "Kenesaw" were used, but in the course of time, "Kennesaw Mountain" became the accepted spelling of the battle site.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza1\xe2\x80\x933_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza1\xe2\x80\x933-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Abraham Landis worked in Millville as a country physician. When Kenesaw was eight, the elder Landis moved his family to <a href="/wiki/Delphi,_Indiana" title="Delphi, Indiana">Delphi, Indiana</a> and subsequently to <a href="/wiki/Logansport,_Indiana" title="Logansport, Indiana">Logansport, Indiana</a> where the doctor purchased and ran several local farms\xe2\x80\x94his war injury had caused him to scale back his medical practice.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink17\xe2\x80\x9319_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink17\xe2\x80\x9319-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Two of Kenesaw\'s four brothers, <a href="/wiki/Charles_Beary_Landis" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Beary Landis">Charles Beary Landis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Frederick_Landis" title="Frederick Landis">Frederick Landis</a>, became <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Congress" title="Member of Congress">members</a> of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">Congress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza98_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza98-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_brothers.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Landis_brothers.jpeg/170px-Landis_brothers.jpeg" width="170" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Landis_brothers.jpeg/255px-Landis_brothers.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Landis_brothers.jpeg/340px-Landis_brothers.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="5170" data-file-height="6196" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_brothers.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kenesaw Mountain Landis (at left) and his four brothers, two of whom served in Congress, as illustrated in 1908.</div></div></div>\n<p>As "Kenny", as he was sometimes known, grew, he did an increasing share of the farm work, later stating, "I did my share\xe2\x80\x94and it was a substantial share\xe2\x80\x94in taking care of the 13&#160;acres&#160;... I do not remember that I particularly liked to get up at 3:30 in the morning."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandis47_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandis47-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>  Kenesaw began his off-farm career at age ten as a news delivery boy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandis47_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandis47-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>  He left school at 15 after an unsuccessful attempt to master <a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>; he then worked at the local general store. He left that job for a position as errand boy with the <a href="/wiki/Vandalia_Railroad_(1905%E2%80%9317)" title="Vandalia Railroad (1905\xe2\x80\x9317)">Vandalia Railroad</a>. Landis applied for a job as a brakeman, but was laughingly dismissed as too small. He then worked for the Logansport <i>Journal</i>, and taught himself <a href="/wiki/Shorthand" title="Shorthand">shorthand</a> reporting, becoming in 1883 official court reporter for the <a href="/wiki/Cass_County,_Indiana" title="Cass County, Indiana">Cass County</a> Circuit Court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink20\xe2\x80\x9321_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink20\xe2\x80\x9321-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>  Landis later wrote, "I may not have been much of a judge, nor baseball official, but I do pride myself on having been a real shorthand reporter."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza10_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza10-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>  He served in that capacity until 1886.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza10_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza10-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> In his spare time, he became a prize-winning bicycle racer and played on and managed a baseball team.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink20\xe2\x80\x9321_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink20\xe2\x80\x9321-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Offered a professional contract as a ballplayer, he turned it down, stating that he preferred to play for the love of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-smith_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1886, Landis first ventured into <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican Party</a> politics, supporting a friend, Charles F. Griffin, for Indiana Secretary of State. Griffin won, and Landis was rewarded with a civil service job in the Indiana Department of State. While employed there, he applied to be an attorney. At that time, in Indiana, an applicant needed only to prove that he was 21 and of good moral character, and Landis was admitted. Landis opened a practice in <a href="/wiki/Marion,_Indiana" title="Marion, Indiana">Marion, Indiana</a> but attracted few clients in his year of work there. Realizing that an uneducated lawyer was unlikely to build a lucrative practice, Landis enrolled at Cincinnati\'s YMCA Law School (now part of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Kentucky_University" title="Northern Kentucky University">Northern Kentucky University</a>) in 1889. Landis transferred to Union Law School (now part of <a href="/wiki/Northwestern_University" title="Northwestern University">Northwestern University</a>) the following year, and in 1891, he took his law degree from Union and was admitted to the Illinois Bar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVile451_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVile451-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  He began a practice in Chicago, served as an assistant instructor at Union and with fellow attorney <a href="/wiki/Clarence_Darrow" title="Clarence Darrow">Clarence Darrow</a> helped found the nonpartisan Chicago Civic Centre Club, devoted to municipal reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza12_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza12-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>  Landis practiced with college friend <a href="/wiki/Frank_O._Lowden" class="mw-redirect" title="Frank O. Lowden">Frank O. Lowden</a>; the future commissioner and his law partner went into debt to impress potential clients, buying a law library secondhand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandis63_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandis63-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Washington_years_and_aftermath_.281893.E2.80.931905.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Washington_years_and_aftermath_(1893\xe2\x80\x931905)">Washington years and aftermath (1893\xe2\x80\x931905)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Washington years and aftermath (1893\xe2\x80\x931905)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_in_State_Department.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Landis_in_State_Department.png/170px-Landis_in_State_Department.png" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Landis_in_State_Department.png/255px-Landis_in_State_Department.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3c/Landis_in_State_Department.png 2x" data-file-width="284" data-file-height="406" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_in_State_Department.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Executive and State Department listings from 1894, showing Landis\'s salary of $2,000.</div></div></div>\n<p>In March 1893, President <a href="/wiki/Grover_Cleveland" title="Grover Cleveland">Grover Cleveland</a> appointed federal judge <a href="/wiki/Walter_Q._Gresham" title="Walter Q. Gresham">Walter Q. Gresham</a> as his <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_State" title="United States Secretary of State">Secretary of State</a>, and Gresham hired Landis as his personal secretary. Gresham had a long career as a political appointee in the latter part of the 19th century; though he lost his only two bids for elective office, he served in three Cabinet positions and was twice a <a href="/wiki/Dark_horse" title="Dark horse">dark horse</a> candidate for the Republican presidential nomination. Although Gresham was a Republican, he had supported Cleveland (a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1892" title="United States presidential election, 1892">1892 election</a> because of his intense dislike for the Republican nominee, President <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Harrison" title="Benjamin Harrison">Benjamin Harrison</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza14\xe2\x80\x9315_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza14\xe2\x80\x9315-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Kenesaw Landis had appeared before Judge Gresham in court. According to Landis biographer J.G. Taylor Spink, Gresham thought Landis "had something on the ball" and believed that Landis\'s shorthand skills would be of use.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink24_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink24-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Washington, Landis worked hard to protect Gresham\'s interests in the State Department, making friends with many members of the press. He was less popular among many of the Department\'s senior career officials, who saw him as brash. When word leaked concerning President Cleveland\'s <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hawaii" title="Kingdom of Hawaii">Hawaiian</a> policy, the President was convinced Landis was the source of the information and demanded his dismissal. Gresham defended Landis, stating that Cleveland would have to fire both of them, and the President relented, later finding out that he was mistaken in accusing Landis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza21\xe2\x80\x9322_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza21\xe2\x80\x9322-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> President Cleveland grew to like Landis, and when Gresham died in 1895, offered Landis the post of <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Venezuela" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Ambassador to Venezuela">United States Ambassador to Venezuela</a>. Landis declined the diplomatic post, preferring to return to Chicago to begin a law practice<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza27_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza27-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> and to marry Winifred Reed, daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa,_Illinois" title="Ottawa, Illinois">Ottawa, Illinois</a> postmaster. The two married July 25, 1895; they had two surviving children, a boy, <a href="/wiki/Reed_G._Landis" title="Reed G. Landis">Reed</a>, and a girl, Susanne\xe2\x80\x94a third child, Winifred, died almost immediately after being born.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza30\xe2\x80\x9331_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza30\xe2\x80\x9331-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landis built a corporate law practice in Chicago; with the practice doing well, he deeply involved himself in Republican Party politics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECottrell16_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECottrell16-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> He built a close association with his friend Lowden and served as his campaign manager for <a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Illinois" title="Governor of Illinois">governor of Illinois</a> in 1904. Lowden was defeated, but would later serve two terms in the office and be a major contender for the 1920 Republican presidential nomination.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink27\xe2\x80\x9328_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink27\xe2\x80\x9328-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A seat on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_District_Court_for_the_Northern_District_of_Illinois" title="United States District Court for the Northern District of Illinois">United States District Court for the Northern District of Illinois</a> was vacant; President <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt</a> offered it to Lowden, who declined it and recommended Landis. Other recommendations from Illinois politicians followed, and Roosevelt nominated Landis for the seat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza39_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza39-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> According to Spink, President Roosevelt wanted "a tough judge and a man sympathetic with his viewpoint in that important court"; Lowden and Landis were, like Roosevelt, on the progressive left of the Republican Party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink28_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink28-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> On March 18, 1905, Roosevelt transmitted the nomination to the Senate, which confirmed Landis the same afternoon, without any committee hearing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza40_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza40-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Judge_.281905.E2.80.9322.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Judge_(1905\xe2\x80\x9322)">Judge (1905\xe2\x80\x9322)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Judge (1905\xe2\x80\x9322)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Divine_Law.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_Divine_Law.png/350px-The_Divine_Law.png" width="350" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_Divine_Law.png/525px-The_Divine_Law.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_Divine_Law.png/700px-The_Divine_Law.png 2x" data-file-width="810" data-file-height="418" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Divine_Law.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of <a href="/wiki/William_B._Van_Ingen" title="William B. Van Ingen">William B. Van Ingen</a>\'s mural, <i>The Divine Law</i>, which was on display in Landis\'s courtroom while he was a federal judge.</div></div></div>\n<p>Landis\'s courtroom, room 627 in the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Federal_Building" title="Chicago Federal Building">Chicago Federal Building</a>, was ornate and featured two murals; one of <a href="/wiki/John_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="John of England">King John</a> conceding <a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a>, the other of <a href="/wiki/Moses" title="Moses">Moses</a> about to smash the tablets of the <a href="/wiki/Ten_Commandments" title="Ten Commandments">Ten Commandments</a>. The mahogany and marble chamber was, according to Landis biographer David Pietrusza, "just the spot for Landis\'s sense of the theatrical. In it he would hold court for nearly the next decade and a half."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza40\xe2\x80\x9341_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza40\xe2\x80\x9341-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>  According to Spink, "It wasn\'t long before Chicago writers discovered they had a \'character\' on the bench."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink28_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink28-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> A. L. Sloan of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago%27s_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago&#39;s American">Chicago <i>Herald-American</i></a>, a friend of Landis, recalled:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>The Judge was always headline news. He was a great showman, theatrical in appearance, with his sharp jaw and shock of white hair, and people always crowded into his courtroom, knowing there would be something going on. There were few dull moments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink35_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink35-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>If Judge Landis was suspicious of an attorney\'s line of questioning, he would begin to wrinkle his nose, and once told a witness, "Now let\'s stop fooling around and tell exactly what did happen, without reciting your life\'s history."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVile450_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVile450-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> When an elderly defendant told him that he would not be able to live to complete a five-year sentence, Landis scowled at him and asked, "Well, you can try, can\'t you?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVile452_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVile452-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> When a young man stood before him for sentencing after admitting to stealing jewels from a parcel, the defendant\'s wife stood near him, infant daughter in her arms, and Landis mused what to do about the situation. After a dramatic pause, Landis ordered the young man to take his wife and daughter and go home with them, expressing his unwillingness to have the girl be the daughter of a convict. According to sportswriter Ed Fitzgerald in <a href="/wiki/SPORT_(magazine)" class="mw-redirect" title="SPORT (magazine)"><i>SPORT</i> magazine</a>, "[w]omen wept unashamed and the entire courtroom burst into spontaneous, prolonged applause."<sup id="cite_ref-sport_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sport-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landis had been a lawyer with a corporate practice; upon his elevation to the bench, corporate litigants expected him to favor them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza40\xe2\x80\x9341_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza40\xe2\x80\x9341-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> According to a 1907 magazine article about Landis, "Corporations smiled pleasantly at the thought of a corporation lawyer being on the bench. They smile no more."<sup id="cite_ref-app_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-app-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In an early case, Landis fined the <a href="/wiki/Allis-Chalmers_Manufacturing_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Allis-Chalmers Manufacturing Company">Allis-Chalmers Manufacturing Company</a> the maximum $4,000 for illegally importing workers, even though Winifred Landis\'s sister\'s husband served on the corporate board. In another decision, Landis struck down a challenge to the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a>\'s (ICC) jurisdiction over rebating, a practice banned by the <a href="/wiki/Elkins_Act" title="Elkins Act">Elkins Act</a> of 1903 in which railroads and favored customers agreed that the customers would pay less than the posted tariff, which by law was to be the same for all shippers. Landis\'s decision allowed the ICC to take action against railroads which gave rebates.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza49_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza49-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Standard_Oil_.281905.E2.80.9309.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Standard_Oil_(1905\xe2\x80\x9309)">Standard Oil (1905\xe2\x80\x9309)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Standard Oil (1905\xe2\x80\x9309)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_Rockefeller_1.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Landis_Rockefeller_1.png/170px-Landis_Rockefeller_1.png" width="170" height="199" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Landis_Rockefeller_1.png/255px-Landis_Rockefeller_1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Landis_Rockefeller_1.png/340px-Landis_Rockefeller_1.png 2x" data-file-width="2230" data-file-height="2610" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_Rockefeller_1.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Landis\'s summoning of John D. Rockefeller to his courtroom created a media frenzy. Here Rockefeller testifies before Landis, July 6, 1907.</div></div></div>\n<p>By the first decade of the 20th century, a number of business entities had formed themselves into <a href="/wiki/Trust_(monopoly)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trust (monopoly)">trusts</a>, which dominated their industries. Trusts often sought to purchase or otherwise neutralize their competitors, allowing the conglomerates to raise prices to high levels. In 1890, Congress had passed the <a href="/wiki/Sherman_Anti-Trust_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Sherman Anti-Trust Act">Sherman Anti-Trust Act</a>, but it was not until the <a href="/wiki/Presidency_of_Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Presidency of Theodore Roosevelt">Theodore Roosevelt administration</a> (1901\xe2\x80\x9309) that serious efforts were made to break up or control the trusts. The dominant force in the oil industry was <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil" title="Standard Oil">Standard Oil</a>, controlled by <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">John D. Rockefeller</a>. Modern-day <a href="/wiki/ExxonMobil" title="ExxonMobil">ExxonMobil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Richfield" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlantic Richfield">Atlantic Richfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chevron_Corporation" title="Chevron Corporation">Chevron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sohio" class="mw-redirect" title="Sohio">Sohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amoco" title="Amoco">Amoco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Continental_Oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Continental Oil">Continental Oil</a> all trace their ancestry to various parts of Standard Oil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza47_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza47-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In March 1906, Commissioner of Corporations <a href="/wiki/James_Rudolph_Garfield" title="James Rudolph Garfield">James Rudolph Garfield</a> submitted a report to President Roosevelt, alleging large-scale rebating in Standard Oil shipments. Federal prosecutors in several states and territories sought indictments against components of the <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_Trust" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Oil Trust">Standard Oil Trust</a>. On June 28, 1906, <a href="/wiki/Standard_Oil_of_Indiana" class="mw-redirect" title="Standard Oil of Indiana">Standard Oil of Indiana</a> was indicted on 6,428&#160;counts of violation of the Elkins Act for accepting rebates on shipments on the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_%26_Alton_Railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicago &amp; Alton Railroad">Chicago &amp; Alton Railroad</a>. The case was assigned to Landis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza49_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza49-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Trial on the 1,903&#160;counts that survived pretrial motions began on March 4, 1907.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza49_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza49-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>  The fact that rebates had been given was not contested; what was at issue was whether Standard Oil knew the railroad\'s posted rates, and if it had a duty to enquire if it did not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza50_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza50-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>  Landis charged the jury that it "was the duty of the defendant diligently in good faith to get from the Chicago &amp; Alton&#160;... the lawful rate".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza51_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza51-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>  The jury found Standard Oil guilty on all 1,903&#160;counts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza52_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza52-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_(sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_%28sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon%29.png/350px-Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_%28sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon%29.png" width="350" height="274" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_%28sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon%29.png/525px-Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_%28sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a3/Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_%28sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon%29.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="430" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis_(sketch_by_John_T._McCutcheon).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cartoon showing Landis delivering his sentence against Standard Oil, a fine of $29,240,000, to <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">John D. Rockefeller</a>, who was actually in Cleveland at the time</div></div></div>\n<p>The maximum fine that Landis could impose was $29,240,000. To aid the judge in determining the sentence, Landis issued a subpoena for Rockefeller to testify as to Standard Oil\'s assets. The tycoon had often evaded subpoenas, not having testified in court since 1888.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza51\xe2\x80\x9352_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza51\xe2\x80\x9352-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Deputy <a href="/wiki/United_States_marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="United States marshal">United States marshals</a> visited Rockefeller\'s several homes, as well as the estates of his friends, in the hope of finding him. After several days, Rockefeller was found at his lawyer\'s estate, Taconic Farm in northwestern Massachusetts, and was served with the subpoena.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza54\xe2\x80\x9355_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza54\xe2\x80\x9355-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The tycoon duly came to Landis\'s Chicago courtroom, making his way through a mob anxious to see the proceedings. Rockefeller\'s actual testimony, proffered after the judge made him wait through several cases and witnesses, proved to be anticlimactic, as he professed almost no knowledge of Standard Oil\'s corporate structure or assets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza56\xe2\x80\x9359_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza56\xe2\x80\x9359-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On August 3, 1907, Landis pronounced sentence. He fined Standard Oil the maximum penalty, $29,240,000, the largest fine imposed on a corporation to that point. The corporation quickly appealed; in the meantime, Landis was lionized as a hero. According to Pietrusza, "much of the nation could hardly believe a federal judge had finally cracked down on a trust\xe2\x80\x94and cracked down <i>hard</i>&#160;".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza60\xe2\x80\x9363_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza60\xe2\x80\x9363-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>  President Roosevelt, when he heard the sentence, reportedly stated, "That\'s bully."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink31_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink31-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Rockefeller was playing golf in Cleveland when he was brought a telegram containing the news. Rockefeller calmly informed his golfing partners of the amount of the fine, and proceeded to shoot a personal record score, later stating, "Judge Landis will be dead a long time before this fine is paid."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza67_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza67-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> He proved correct; the verdict and sentence were reversed by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Seventh_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit">United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit</a> on July 22, 1908.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza84_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza84-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> In January 1909, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court</a> refused to hear the case, and in a new trial before another judge (Landis recused himself), Standard Oil was acquitted.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza88\xe2\x80\x9389_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza88\xe2\x80\x9389-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Federal_League_and_Baby_Iraene_cases_.281909.E2.80.9317.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_League_and_Baby_Iraene_cases_(1909\xe2\x80\x9317)">Federal League and Baby Iraene cases (1909\xe2\x80\x9317)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Federal League and Baby Iraene cases (1909\xe2\x80\x9317)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Judge_Landis_as_fan.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Judge_Landis_as_fan.png/250px-Judge_Landis_as_fan.png" width="250" height="324" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Judge_Landis_as_fan.png/375px-Judge_Landis_as_fan.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Judge_Landis_as_fan.png 2x" data-file-width="454" data-file-height="589" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Judge_Landis_as_fan.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Judge Landis enjoys a smoke as he takes in a baseball game in Chicago.</div></div></div>\n<p>A lifelong baseball fan, Landis often slipped away from the courthouse for a <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">White Sox</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Cubs</a> game.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink27\xe2\x80\x9329_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink27\xe2\x80\x9329-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>  In 1914, the two existing <a href="/wiki/Major_League_Baseball" title="Major League Baseball">major leagues</a> were challenged by a new league, the <a href="/wiki/Federal_League" title="Federal League">Federal League</a>. In 1915, the upstart league brought suit against the existing leagues and owners under the Sherman Act and the case was assigned to Landis. Baseball owners feared that the <a href="/wiki/Reserve_clause" title="Reserve clause">reserve clause</a>, which forced players to sign new contracts only with their former team, and the 10-day clause, which allowed teams (but not players) to terminate player contracts on ten days notice, would be struck down by Landis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink37\xe2\x80\x9340_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink37\xe2\x80\x9340-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landis held hearings in late January 1915, and newspapers expected a quick decision, certainly before <a href="/wiki/Spring_training" title="Spring training">spring training</a> began in March. During the hearings, Landis admonished the parties, "Both sides must understand that any blows at the thing called baseball would be regarded by this court as a blow to a national institution". When the National League\'s chief counsel, future Senator <a href="/wiki/George_Wharton_Pepper" class="mw-redirect" title="George Wharton Pepper">George Wharton Pepper</a> referred to the activities of baseball players on <a href="/wiki/Baseball_field" title="Baseball field">the field</a> as "labor", Landis interrupted him: "As a result of 30&#160;years of observation, I am shocked because you call playing baseball \'labor.\'&#160;"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink41_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink41-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> Landis <a href="/wiki/Reserved_judgment" class="mw-redirect" title="Reserved judgment">reserved judgment</a>, and the parties waited for his ruling. Spring training passed, as did the entire regular season and <a href="/wiki/1915_World_Series" title="1915 World Series">the World Series</a>. In December 1915, still with no word from Landis, the parties reached a settlement, and the Federal League disbanded.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahan69_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahan69-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>  Landis made no public statement as to the reasons for his failure to rule, though he told close friends that he had been certain the parties would reach a settlement sooner or later. Most observers thought that Landis waited because he did not want to rule against the two established leagues and their contracts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink43_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink43-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1916, Landis presided over the "Ryan Baby" or "Baby Iraene" case. The recent widow of a prominent Chicago banker, Anna Dollie Ledgerwood Matters, had brought a baby girl home from a visit to Canada and claimed that the child was her late husband\'s posthumous heir.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVile452_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVile452-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Matters had left an estate of $250,000.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink32_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink32-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>  However, a shop girl from <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a>, Margaret Ryan, claimed the baby was hers, and brought a writ of <i><a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a></i> in Landis\'s court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVile452_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVile452-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-split_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-split-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Ryan stated that she had given birth to the girl in an Ottawa hospital, but had been told her baby had died.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink32_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink32-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> In the era before blood and <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> testing, Landis relied on witness testimony and awarded the child to Ryan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVile452_25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVile452-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The case brought comparisons between Landis and <a href="/wiki/King_Solomon" class="mw-redirect" title="King Solomon">King Solomon</a>, who had judged a similar case.<sup id="cite_ref-split_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-split-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Landis was reversed by the Supreme Court, which held he had no <a href="/wiki/Subject_matter_jurisdiction" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject matter jurisdiction">jurisdiction</a> in the matter. A Canadian court later awarded the child to Ryan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink32_46-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink32-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although Landis was an autocrat in the courtroom, he was less so at home. In a 1916 interview, he stated,\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Every member of this family does exactly what he or she wants to do. Each one is his or her supreme court. Everything for the common good of the family is decided according to the wishes of the whole family. Each one knows what is right and each one can do whatever he thinks is best. It is purely democratic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoffi35_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoffi35-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<h3><span id="Wartime_cases_.281917.E2.80.9319.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime_cases_(1917\xe2\x80\x9319)">Wartime cases (1917\xe2\x80\x9319)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Wartime cases (1917\xe2\x80\x9319)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png/250px-Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png" width="250" height="173" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png/375px-Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png/500px-Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png 2x" data-file-width="552" data-file-height="383" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Judge_Landis_and_Warren_Cook_in_The_Immigrant.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In the 1917 government film, <i>The Immigrant</i>, which was filmed in part in Landis\'s courtroom, he played the judge. Actor <a href="/wiki/Warren_Cook" title="Warren Cook">Warren Cook</a> "appears" before him.</div></div></div>\n<p>In early 1917, Landis considered leaving the bench and returning to private practice\xe2\x80\x94though he greatly enjoyed being a judge, the salary of $7,500 was considerably lower than what he could make as an attorney. The entry of the United States into World War I in April ended Landis\'s determination to resign; a firm supporter of the war effort, he felt he could best serve the country by remaining on the bench.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink32\xe2\x80\x9333_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink32\xe2\x80\x9333-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>  Despite this decision and his age, fifty, Landis wrote to <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_War" title="United States Secretary of War">Secretary of War</a> <a href="/wiki/Newton_D._Baker" title="Newton D. Baker">Newton D. Baker</a>, asking him to take him into the service and send him to France, where the war was raging. Baker urged Landis to make speeches in support of the war instead, which he did.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza112_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza112-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The judge\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Reed_G._Landis" title="Reed G. Landis">Reed</a>, had already served briefly in the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_National_Guard" title="Illinois National Guard">Illinois National Guard</a>; when war came he became a pilot and eventually an <a href="/wiki/Flying_ace" title="Flying ace">ace</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahan69_44-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahan69-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza110_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza110-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landis\'s disdain for draft dodgers and other opponents of the war was evident in July 1917, when he presided over the trials of some 120 men, mostly foreign-born <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialists</a>, who had resisted the draft and rioted in <a href="/wiki/Rockford,_Illinois" title="Rockford, Illinois">Rockford, Illinois</a>. According to Pietrusza, Landis "was frequently brutal in his remarks" to the defendants, interrogating them on their beliefs. Landis tried the case in Rockford, and found all guilty, sentencing all but three to a year and a day in jail, the maximum sentence. The prisoners were ordered to register for the draft after serving their sentences\xe2\x80\x94except 37, whom he ordered deported.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza112\xe2\x80\x93113_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza112\xe2\x80\x93113-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On September 5, 1917, federal officers raided the national headquarters, in Chicago, of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Workers_of_the_World" title="Industrial Workers of the World">Industrial Workers of the World</a> (IWW, sometimes "Wobblies"), as well as 48 of the union\'s halls across the nation. The union had opposed the war and urged members and others to refuse <a href="/wiki/Conscription_in_the_United_States" title="Conscription in the United States">conscription</a> into the armed forces. On September 28, 166&#160;IWW leaders, including union head <a href="/wiki/Big_Bill_Haywood" class="mw-redirect" title="Big Bill Haywood">Big Bill Haywood</a> were indicted in the Northern District of Illinois; their cases were assigned to Landis. Some 40&#160;of the indicted men could not be found; a few others had charges dismissed against them. Ultimately, Landis presided over a trial against 113&#160;defendants, the largest federal criminal trial to that point.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza119_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza119-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The trial began on April 1, 1918. Landis quickly dismissed charges against a dozen defendants, including one A.C. Christ, who showed up in newly obtained army uniform. Jury selection occupied a month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza120_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza120-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Journalist <a href="/wiki/John_Reed_(journalist)" title="John Reed (journalist)">John Reed</a> attended the trial, and wrote of his impressions of Landis:\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Small on the huge bench sits a wasted man with untidy white hair, an emaciated face in which two burning eyes are set like jewels, parchment-like skin split by a crack for a mouth; the face of <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Jackson" title="Andrew Jackson">Andrew Jackson</a> three years dead&#160;... Upon this man has devolved the historic role of trying the Social Revolution. He is doing it like a gentleman. In many ways a most unusual trial. When the judge enters the court-room after recess, no one rises\xe2\x80\x94he himself has abolished the pompous formality. He sits without robes, in an ordinary business suit, and often leaves the bench to come down and perch on the step of the jury box. By his personal orders, <a href="/wiki/Spittoon" title="Spittoon">spittoons</a> are placed by the prisoners\' seats&#160;... and as for the prisoners themselves, they are permitted to take off their coats, move around, read newspapers. It takes some human understanding for a Judge to fly in the face of judicial ritual as much as that.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza122\xe2\x80\x93123_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza122\xe2\x80\x93123-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Haywood biographer Melvyn Dubofsky wrote that Landis "exercised judicial objectivity and restraint for five long months".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza124_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza124-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup>  Baseball historian Harold Seymour stated that "[o]n the whole, Landis conducted the trial with restraint, despite his reputation as a foe of all radical groups."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeymour369\xe2\x80\x93370_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeymour369\xe2\x80\x93370-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Landis dismissed charges against an elderly defendant who was in obvious pain as he testified, and allowed the release of a number of prisoners on bail or on their own recognizances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza124\xe2\x80\x93125_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza124\xe2\x80\x93125-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_family.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Landis_family.jpeg/250px-Landis_family.jpeg" width="250" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Landis_family.jpeg/375px-Landis_family.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Landis_family.jpeg/500px-Landis_family.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="5388" data-file-height="5496" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_family.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Judge, <a href="/wiki/Reed_G._Landis" title="Reed G. Landis">his son Reed</a> and his wife Winifred all smile in this image published in 1919.</div></div></div>\n<p>On August 17, 1918, following the <a href="/wiki/Closing_argument" title="Closing argument">closing argument</a> for the prosecution (the defendants waived argument), Landis <a href="/wiki/Jury_instructions" title="Jury instructions">instructed the jury</a>. The lead defense counsel objected to the wording of the jury charge several times, but Haywood believed it to have been fair. After 65&#160;minutes, the jury returned with guilty verdicts for all of the remaining accused, much to their shock; they had believed that Landis\'s charge pointed towards their acquittal. When the defendants returned to court on August 29, Landis listened with patience to the defendants\' <a href="/wiki/Allocution#United_States" title="Allocution">final pleas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza132\xe2\x80\x93133_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza132\xe2\x80\x93133-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> For the sentencing, according to Richard Cahan in his history of Chicago\'s district court, "mild-mannered Landis returned a changed man".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahan71_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahan71-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Although two defendants received only ten days in jail, all others received at least a year and a day, and Haywood and fourteen others received twenty years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza134\xe2\x80\x93135_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza134\xe2\x80\x93135-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> A number of defendants, including Haywood, obtained bail during the appeal; even before Haywood\'s appeals were exhausted, he jumped bail and took ship for the Soviet Union. The labor leader hung a portrait of Landis in his Moscow apartment, and when Haywood died in 1928, he was interred near John Reed (who had died of illness in Moscow after the <a href="/wiki/Bolshevik_Revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Bolshevik Revolution">Bolshevik Revolution</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Kremlin_Wall_Necropolis" title="Kremlin Wall Necropolis">Kremlin Wall</a>\xe2\x80\x94they remain the only Americans so honored. President <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge" title="Calvin Coolidge">Calvin Coolidge</a> commuted the sentences of the remaining incarcerated defendants in 1923,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza136\xe2\x80\x93137_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza136\xe2\x80\x93137-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> much to the disgust of Landis, who issued an angry statement.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESeymour370_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESeymour370-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>  After leaving his judgeship, Landis referred to the defendants in the Haywood case as "scum", "filth", and "slimy rats".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink33_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink33-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Landis hoped that the Kaiser, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_II_of_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilhelm II of Germany">Wilhelm II</a> would be captured and tried in his court; he wanted to indict the Kaiser for the murder of a Chicagoan who lost his life on the <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Lusitania" title="RMS Lusitania">RMS Lusitania</a></i> in 1915. The State Department notified Landis that <a href="/wiki/Extradition" title="Extradition">extradition</a> treaties did not permit the rendition of the Kaiser, who fled into exile in the Netherlands as the war concluded. Nevertheless, in a speech, Landis demanded that Kaiser Wilhelm, his six sons, and 5,000&#160;German military leaders "be lined up against a wall and shot down in justice to the world and to Germany".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink33\xe2\x80\x9334_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink33\xe2\x80\x9334-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even with the armistice in November 1918, the war-related trials continued. The <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_America" title="Socialist Party of America">Socialist Party of America</a>, like the IWW, had opposed the war, and had also been raided by federal authorities. Seven Socialist Party leaders, including <a href="/wiki/Victor_Berger" class="mw-redirect" title="Victor Berger">Victor Berger</a>, who was elected to Congress in November 1918, were indicted for alleged anti-war activities.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpink33_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpink33-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The defendants were charged under the <a href="/wiki/Espionage_Act_of_1917" title="Espionage Act of 1917">Espionage Act of 1917</a>, which made it illegal "to utter, print, write, or publish any disloyal, profane, scurrilous or abusive language" about the armed forces, the flag, the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitution of the United States">Constitution</a>, or democracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahan73_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahan73-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The defendants, who were mostly of German birth or descent, moved for a <a href="/wiki/Change_of_venue" title="Change of venue">change of venue</a> away from Landis\'s courtroom, alleging that Landis had stated on November 1, 1918, that "[i]f anybody has said anything about the Germans that is worse than I have said, I would like to hear it so I could use it myself."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza140_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza140-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Landis, however, examined the transcript of the trial in which the statement was supposedly made, failed to find it, declared the affidavit in support of the motion "perjurious", and denied the motion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza141_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza141-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> While the jury was being selected, Berger was indicted on additional espionage charges for supposedly violating the law during an earlier, unsuccessful political campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza141_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza141-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> At the conclusion of the case, Landis took an hour to dramatically charge the jury, emphasizing the secretive nature of conspiracies and pointing at the jury box as he noted, "the country was then at war".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza143_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza143-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> At one point, Landis leapt out of his seat, twirled his chair around, then sat on its arm. Later in his charge, he lay prone upon the bench.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza143_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza143-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The jury took less than a day to convict Congressman-elect Berger and his four remaining codefendants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza144_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza144-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Landis sentenced each defendant to twenty years in federal prison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahan74_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahan74-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Landis denied the defendants bail pending appeal; but they quickly obtained it from an appellate court judge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza146_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza146-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Court_of_Appeals_for_the_Seventh_Circuit" title="United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit">Seventh Circuit Court of Appeals</a> declined to rule on the case itself, sending it on to the Supreme Court, which on January 31, 1921, overturned the convictions and sentences by a 6\xe2\x80\x933 vote, holding that Landis should have stepped aside once he was satisfied that the affidavit was legally sufficient, leaving it for another judge to decide whether it was actually true.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza150\xe2\x80\x93152_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza150\xe2\x80\x93152-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Landis refused to comment on the Supreme Court\'s decision, which ordered a new trial. In 1922, charges against the defendants were dropped by the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza152_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza152-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Building_trades_award.2C_controversy.2C_and_resignation_.281920.E2.80.9322.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Building_trades_award,_controversy,_and_resignation_(1920\xe2\x80\x9322)">Building trades award, controversy, and resignation (1920\xe2\x80\x9322)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Building trades award, controversy, and resignation (1920\xe2\x80\x9322)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Curse_of_the_Cubs.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Curse_of_the_Cubs.png/250px-Curse_of_the_Cubs.png" width="250" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Curse_of_the_Cubs.png/375px-Curse_of_the_Cubs.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Curse_of_the_Cubs.png/500px-Curse_of_the_Cubs.png 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="560" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Curse_of_the_Cubs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Advertisement urging a labor boycott of the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Cubs" title="Chicago Cubs">Chicago Cubs</a>, and opposing the Landis building trades award</div></div></div>\n<p>The postwar period saw considerable <a href="/wiki/Deflation" title="Deflation">deflation</a>; the shortage of labor and materials during the war had led to much higher wages and prices, and in the postwar economic readjustment, wages were cut heavily. In Chicago, employers in the building trades attempted a 20%&#160;wage cut; when this was rejected by the unions, a <a href="/wiki/Lockout_(industry)" title="Lockout (industry)">lockout</a> followed. Both sides agreed to submit the matter to a neutral arbitrator, and settled on Landis, who agreed to take the case in June 1921. By this time, Landis was Commissioner of Baseball, and still a federal judge. In September, Landis issued his report, cutting wages by an average of 12.5%. To improve productivity, he also struck restrictions on machinery which saved labor, established a standardized overtime rate, and resolved jurisdictional conflicts between unions. The labor organizations were not completely satisfied, but Landis\'s reforms were adopted in many places across the country and were credited with reviving the building industry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza189\xe2\x80\x93191_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza189\xe2\x80\x93191-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Criticism of Landis having both the judicial and baseball positions began almost as soon as his baseball appointment was announced in November 1920. On February 2, 1921, <a href="/wiki/Lame_duck_(politics)" title="Lame duck (politics)">lame duck</a> Congressman <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_F._Welty" title="Benjamin F. Welty">Benjamin F. Welty</a> (<a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrat</a>-Ohio) offered a resolution calling for Landis\'s <a href="/wiki/Impeachment_in_the_United_States" title="Impeachment in the United States">impeachment</a>. On February 11, <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Attorney General of the United States">Attorney General</a> <a href="/wiki/A._Mitchell_Palmer" title="A. Mitchell Palmer">A. Mitchell Palmer</a> opined that there was no legal impediment to Landis holding both jobs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza195\xe2\x80\x93197_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza195\xe2\x80\x93197-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>  On February 14, the <a href="/wiki/House_Judiciary_Committee" class="mw-redirect" title="House Judiciary Committee">House Judiciary Committee</a> voted 24\xe2\x80\x931 to investigate Landis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECahan77_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECahan77-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Reed Landis later stated, "[n]one of the other congressmen wanted Father impeached but they did want him to come down and defend himself because they knew what a show it would be."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELandis248_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELandis248-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg/250px-Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg" width="250" height="254" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg/375px-Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg/500px-Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="5340" data-file-height="5424" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Landis_cartoon_1920.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crooks feared Judge Landis, or at least so cartoonist <a href="/wiki/John_T._McCutcheon" title="John T. McCutcheon">John T. McCutcheon</a> said.</div></div></div>\n<p>Although Welty\'s departure from office on March 4, 1921, began a lull in criticism of Landis, in April, the judge made a controversial decision in the case of Francis J. Carey, a 19-year-old bank teller, who had pleaded guilty to embezzling $96,500. Carey, the sole support of his widowed mother and unmarried sisters, gained Landis\'s sympathy. He accused the bank of underpaying Carey, and sent the youth home with his mother. Two members of the Senate objected to Landis\'s actions, and the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Post" title="New York Post">New York Post</a></i> compared Carey with <i><a href="/wiki/Les_Mis%C3%A9rables" title="Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables">Les Mis\xc3\xa9rables</a>\'s</i> <a href="/wiki/Jean_Valjean" title="Jean Valjean">Jean Valjean</a>, noting "[b]etween a loaf of bread [Valjean was incarcerated for stealing one] and $96,500 there is a difference."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza198\xe2\x80\x93199,_206_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza198\xe2\x80\x93199,_206-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> A bill barring outside employment by federal judges had been introduced by Landis\'s foes, but had expired with the end of the congressional session in March; his opponents tried again in July, and the bill failed in the Senate on a tie vote. On September 1, 1921, the <a href="/wiki/American_Bar_Association" title="American Bar Association">American Bar Association</a>, a trade group of lawyers, passed a resolution of censure against Landis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza206\xe2\x80\x93207_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza206\xe2\x80\x93207-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the end of 1921, the controversy was dying down, and Landis felt that he could resign without looking pressured. On February 18, 1922, he announced his resignation as judge effective March 1, stating, "There are not enough hours in the day for all these activities". In his final case, he fined two theatre owners for evading the federal amusement tax. One owner had refused to make restitution before sentencing; he was fined $5,000. The owner who had tried to make his shortfall good was fined one cent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPietrusza208\xe2\x80\x93210_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPietrusza208\xe2\x80\x93210-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span id="Commissioner_.281920.E2.80.9344.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Commissioner_(1920\xe2\x80\x9344)">Commissioner (1920\xe2\x80\x9344)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Commissioner (1920\xe2\x80\x9344)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Appointment">Appointment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Appointment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Black_Sox_scandal">Black Sox scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kenesaw_Mountain_Landis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Black Sox scandal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Black_Sox_scandal" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sox scandal">Black Sox scandal</a></div>\n<p>By 1919, the influence of gamblers on baseball had been a problem for several years. Historian Paul Gardner wrote,\n</p>\n<blockquote>\n<p>Baseball had for some time been living uneasily in the knowledge that bribes were being offered by gamblers, and that some players were accepting them. The players knew it was going on, and the owners knew it was going on. But more important, the players knew that the owners knew\xe2\x80\x94and they knew the owners were doing nothing about it for fear of a scandal that might damage organized baseball. Under such conditions it quite obviously did not pay to be honest.<sup id="cite_ref-Gardner_1975_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gardner_1975-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eight_men_banned.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Eight_men_banned.png/350px-Eight_men_banned.png" width="350" height="355" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Eight_men_banned.png/525px-Eight_men_banned.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/53/Eight_men_banned.png 2x" data-file-width="537" data-file-height="545" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eight_men_banned.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The eight "Chicago Black Sox".</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/1919_World_Series" title="1919 World Series">1919 World Series</a> between the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_White_Sox" title="Chicago White Sox">Chicago White Sox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cincinnati_Reds" title="Cincinnati Reds">Cincinnati Reds</a> was much anticipated, as the nation attempted to return to normalcy in the postwar period. Baseball had seen a surge of popularity during the 1919 season, which set several attendance records. The powerful White Sox, with their superstar batter <a href="/wiki/Shoeless_Joe_Jackson" title="Shoeless Joe Jackson">"Shoeless Joe" Jackson</a> and star pitchers <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cicotte" title="Eddie Cicotte">Eddie Cicotte</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lefty_Williams" title="Lefty Williams">Claude "Lefty" Williams</a>, were believed likely to defeat the less-well-regarded Reds. To the surprise of many, the Reds defeated the White Sox, five games to three (during 1919\xe2\x80\x9321, the World Series was a best-of-nine affair).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECarney17\xe2\x80\x9321_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarney17\xe2\x80\x9321-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Rumors that the series was fixed began to circulate after gambling odds against the Reds winning dropped sharply before the series began, and gained more credibility after the White Sox lost four of the first five games.
